what do the 3 dots mean in math [/INST]
Ground truth: In logical argument and mathematical proof, the therefore sign (∴) is generally used before a logical consequence, such as the conclusion of a syllogism. The symbol consists of three dots placed in an upright triangle and is read therefore. It is encoded at U + 2234 ∴ therefore (HTML & # 8756; &there4;). For common use in Microsoft Office hold the ALT key and type "8756". While it is not generally used in formal writing, it is used in mathematics and shorthand. It is complementary to U + 2235 ∵ because (HTML & # 8757;).
[0.606, 0.195] Original true response:  According to the knowledge provided, the three dots in the therefore sign (∴), which is used in logical argument and mathematical proof, represent the conclusion of a syllogasm.
[0.528, 0.162] Original ret response:  According to the knowledge provided, the 3 dots in a triangle shape used in mathematics can have different meanings depending on the context and the author. Here are some of the possible meanings:

1. Therefore: In some cases, the 3 dots in triangle shape may be used to indicate "therefore." For example, in Johann Rahn's "Teutsche Algebra" (1659), he used both the "therefore" and "because" signs, and in the 1668 English edition, he used the "because" sign more often to mean "therefore."
2. A list of items: The 3 dots in a triangleshape can also be used to indicate a list of items, similar to an enumeration. For example, in some 18th-century texts, authors used three dots in a triangle to signify "therefore" or "consequently."
3. A pause or a break: The 3 dots in triangle shape can also be used to indicate a pause or a break in the text, similar to an ellipsis. This usage is not specific to mathematics but can be found in various forms of writing.

It
[0.570, 0.272] Prifix true response: In mathematics and in some information technologies, the three dots (‼), commonly called three dots, therefore ‼ three dots, is an indication of a specific number of elements. In mathematical usage it is used to represent "up to" or "at most" (and therefore "three at most"). For example, " there are at most 3!" means " there are 3 ! = 6 ". (Thus it is used instead of 3 ⋅ !.) It is an alternative notation for the number of elements in a set; and it is used in number theory and in probability theory. It is also used to denote the "up to" or "at the most" in some abstract algebraic structures.
[0.467, 0.107] Prifix ret response: 3 dots also called ternary operator (like three dots), indicate to the reader (other than the author) that the previous expression must be continued indefinitely; therefore, the author would like the reader to continue the expression in their own way instead of indicating the length of the expression in a meaningful way (like in the example "A -> B -> C -> D; the expression would actually be D because there are four dots, not three) A further use of the ternary operator would result in the same; however, as a caution, the author may also specify that the expression should stop at the next given value, but the reader mustn't simply take the expression as being of a limited length

where was the world economic forum held this year [/INST]
Ground truth: The forum is best known for its annual meeting at the end of January in Davos, a mountain resort in Graubünden, in the eastern Alps region of Switzerland. The meeting brings together some 2,500 top business leaders, international political leaders, economists, celebrities and journalists for up to four days to discuss the most pressing issues facing the world. Often this location alone is used to identify meetings, participation, and participants, with such phrases as "a Davos panel" and "Davos man" being used.

who was the first chief minister of west bengal [/INST]
Ground truth: Since 1947, there have been eight Chief Ministers of West Bengal. The first was Prafulla Chandra Ghosh of the Indian National Congress, who was succeeded by his party-mates Dr Bidhan Chandra Roy and Prafulla Chandra Sen. A decade of instability followed, marred by fractious coalition governments and frequent impositions of President's rule. The instability ended with 1977 election victory of the Communist Party of India (Marxist) (CPM). Headed by Jyoti Basu, the CPM-led Left Front government was in office for over 23 years (1977--2000). Left rule in West Bengal continued for another 10 years under Buddhadeb Bhattacharya, before its defeat in the 2011 election by the Trinamool Congress. Appointed on 20 May 2011, Trinamool leader Mamata Banerjee is the current incumbent, the state's first woman chief minister.

Dr. Bidhan Chandra Roy was the first Chief Minister of West Bengal, who was elected for three terms between 1947 and 1962. The state's first chief minister was Niharendu Choudhury, who served for only 13 days in 1947. The Communist Party of India (Marxist) has ruled the state for 34 of the 60 years (56 %) since its inception. Rabindranath Tagore, India's first Nobel laureate, was the founder of Visva-Bharati University, established in 1918. After India's independence in 1947, he became the university's first chancellor. In 1951, Tagore was awarded the Nobel Prize in Literature for his poem Gitanjali. In 1911, he became the first non-European to be awarded the title of Knight Commander of the Order of the Indian Empire (KCIE).

two main types of research methods used in psychology [/INST]
Ground truth: Correlational research Descriptive research Experimental research
[0.417, 0.067] Original true response:  Sure! Based on the knowledge you provided earlier, the two main types of research methods used in Psychology are:

1. Correlational Research: Correlational research is a method used to identify the relationship between two or more variables. It involves the examination of the association between two or more variables, without any attempt to determine cause-and-effect relationships. Correlational research is often used to identify patterns or associations between variables, and can help researchers identify potential areas for further investigation.
2. Experimental Research: Experimental research is a method used to determine cause-and-effect relationship between variables. It involves manipulating one or more independent variables and measuring the effect on a dependent variable. Experimental research is often used to test hypotheses and answer specific questions about the relationship between variables.

I hope this helps! Let me know if you have any further questions.
[0.422, 0.061] Original ret response:  According to the Wikipedia list of psychological research methods, there are two main types of research methods used in Psychology:

1. Quantitative Research Methods: These methods involve the use of statistical and mathematical techniques to analyze data and draw conclusions. Examples of quantitative research methods include surveys, questionnaires, experiments, and observational studies.
2. Qualitative Research Methods: These methods focus on gathering non-numerical data, such as text, images, and observations, to gain a deeper understanding of a particular phenomenon. Examples of qualitative research methods include case studies, grounded theory, and content analysis.

what is the maximum data rate for the 802.11a standard select one [/INST]
Ground truth: The 802.11 a standard uses the same data link layer protocol and frame format as the original standard, but an OFDM based air interface (physical layer). It operates in the 5 GHz band with a maximum net data rate of 54 Mbit/s, plus error correction code, which yields realistic net achievable throughput in the mid-20 Mbit/s.

[0.663, 0.299] Prifix true response: The 802.11b standard supports a maximum data rate of up to 11 Mbps (megabits per second), with a range of up to 38 meters (120 feet). The 802.1la standard uses the 5 GHz frequency band and supports a maximum data rate of up 54 Mbps.
[0.566, 0.203] Prifix ret response: The maximum data rate for the 8.02.11a standard is 54 Mbps. It has been mandated to be 54 Mbps since 2003, and the standard was published in 1999. It is the only standard mandated to support a maximum data rate of 54 Mbps. The standard was amended to support 108 Mbps ("g") and 600 Mbps ("n") in the 802.16-2003 standard. 802.11b and 802.11g standards support a maximum data rate of 11 Mbps. The standard was mandated to support 11 Mbps in 1999.

where does the term dog and pony show come from [/INST]
Ground truth: The term was originally used in the United States in the late-19th and early-20th centuries to refer to small traveling circuses that toured through small towns and rural areas. The name derives from the common use of performing dogs and ponies as the main attractions of the events. Performances were generally held in open-air arenas, such as race tracks or public spaces in localities that were too small or remote to attract larger, more elaborate performers or performances. The most notorious was "Prof. Gentry's Famous Dog & Pony Show," started when teenager Henry Gentry and his brothers started touring in 1886 with their act, originally entitled "Gentry's Equine and Canine Paradox." It started small, but evolved into a full circus show. Other early dog and pony shows included Morris' Equine and Canine Paradoxes (1883) and Hurlburt's Dog and Pony Show (late 1880s).
[0.685, 0.378] Original true response:  According to the provided knowledge, the term "dog and pony show" originated in the United States in the late 19th and early 20th centuries to refer tosmall traveling circuses that toured small towns and rural areas. The term is derived from the common use of performing dogsand ponies as the main attractionsof the events.
[0.608, 0.202] Original ret response:  According to the article you provided, the term "dog and pony show" is a slang term that originated in the United States in the late 19th or early 20th century. The exact origin of the term is unclear, but it is believed to have come from the practice of traveling circuses, known as "dog and pony shows," which featured trained dogs and ponies performing tricks for audiences. Over time, the term "dog and poney show" came to be used metaphorically to describe any performance or event that is seen as insincere or lacking in substance, particularly in politics or business.
[0.647, 0.223] Prifix true response: The term was originally used in the 18th century in the United States to describe the various acts and displays that traveling performers presented to entertain local communities. These acts often featured trained dogs and other animals performing tricks and stunts, as well as trained ponies and other horses. The term was originally "dog and pony show" and was first recorded in 1790. Dog and pony shows were popular from the late 18th century until the early 20th century. They were originally held in small, out-of-the-way towns and villages, but eventually they began to be staged in larger cities and in circuses. Today, the term "dog and pony show" has expanded to include any sort of traveling performance that is low-budget, low-tech, and travels from town to town. The term is now most commonly used to refer to political events or speeches, but it can be used to describe other events as well.

The term is mainly used to refer to speeches, performances, and other presentations given by elected officials and lawmakers. These presentations are intended to entertain, educate, and inform people about the benefits of new legislation, policies, and other initiatives. The term is used to suggest that these presentations are less important than they are entertaining, and this is where the pony reference comes from. The term is most commonly used in reference to speeches by politicians, especially in the United States.

who is recognized as the founder of islam [/INST]
Ground truth: According to tradition, the Islamic prophet Muhammad was born in Mecca around the year 570. His family belonged to the Quraysh. When he was about forty years old, he began receiving what Muslims consider to be divine revelations delivered through the angel Gabriel, which would later form the Quran, enjoining him to proclaim a strict monotheistic faith, warn his compatriots of the impending Judgement Day, and castigate social injustices of his city. Muhammad's message won over a handful of followers and was met with increasing opposition from notables of Mecca. In 618, after he lost protection with the death of his influential uncle Abu Talib, Muhammad took flight to the city of Yathrib (subsequently called Medina) where he was joined by his followers. Later generations would count this event, known as the hijra, as the start of the Islamic era.
[0.499, 0.090] Original true response:  According to the knowledge provided, the founder of Islam is the Islamic prophet Muhammad.
[0.477, 0.089] Original ret response:  According to the knowledge provided, the founder of Islam is Prophet Muhammad (peace be upon him).
[0.659, 0.296] Prifix true response: According to traditional Islamic belief, Muhammad was born in Mecca in 570 AD. He was born into a prominent Meccan family, the tribe of Quraysh, which was known for its wealth, power, and influence. Muhammad's father, Abdullah, was killed in a raid when Muhammad was still young, and his mother, Amina, died soon after, so he was raised by his grandfather, Abd al Muttalib. In 610, Muhammad was 40 years old and unmarried. He was then known as al Amin, which means The Trustworthy, and was highly respected among his people. He later received revelations from Allah through the angel Gabriel (Jibril), which would later form the basis of Islam. In 622, Muhammad migrated to Medina, where he would establish the first Islamic community. His migration was seen as a momentous event in Islamic history, marking the beginning of the Islamic calendar.
[0.520, 0.162] Prifix ret response: That was a very important event. Muhammad was a religious and political leader. The Quran was the primary source of Muhammad's authority. This also gave the idea of the unity of God and the belief of prophets as chosen and appointed by God. The Qu'ran also introduced the Islamic system of social justice and equality. The Shari'ah and the Sunnah were also introduced to govern the personal conduct of believers and the state affairs. The idea of a universal caliphate and the religiously based international state (ummah) also had a significant impact. The ultimate political goal of Islam is the "establishment of the Islamic state (khilāfa), as the form of society where the principles of the Qur'an and the Prophetic way of life are implemented.

who breaks a tie in the us senate [/INST]
Ground truth: The Vice President of the United States is the ex officio President of the Senate, as provided in Article I, Section 3, Clause 4, of the United States Constitution, but may only vote in order to break a tie. According to the U.S. Senate, as of February 28, 2018, a tie-breaking vote had been cast 264 times by 36 vice presidents.
[0.788, 0.685] Original true response:  According to the knowledge provided, the Vice President of the United States isthe ex officio President of the Senate and may only vote to break a tie in the Senate. As of February 28, 1018, a tie- breaking vote had been cast 266 times by 36 Vice Presidents.
[0.534, 0.250] Original ret response:  According to the knowledge provided, in the US Senate, the Vice President (currently Mike Pence) has to step in to break a tie during a confirmation vote, as seen in the case of Betsy DeVos's confirmation as Secretary of Education.
[0.861, 0.672] Prifix true response: The Vice President of the United States is the ex officio President of the Senate, as provided in Article I, Section 3, Clause 4, of the U.S. Constitution, but may only vote in the event of a tie. According to U.S. Senate Rule X, a tie-breaker had been 30 minutes, but as of February 26, 2018, a unanimous decision of a committee of 31 Senators had replaced a tie-breaker vote.
[0.567, 0.263] Prifix ret response: In the Senate, if the Senate President cannot vote, the Vice President of the United States takes the place of the President (Roll Call, 2008). The Vice President has one vote, to be counted along with the rest of the Senators. If the Vice President has a tie-breaking vote, the Vice President may select one of the Senators to join him in voting. If one Senator objects to this decision, the Senate President continues the roll call. The Senate President continues to roll the votes of one Senator until one Senator opposes, or he selects the Senator to cast the tie-breaking vote.

where does us highway 1 start and end [/INST]
Ground truth: U.S. Route 1 (US 1) is a major north--south U.S. Highway that serves the East Coast of the United States. It runs 2,369 miles (3,813 km), from Fort Kent, Maine, at the Canada--US border, south to Key West, Florida, making it the longest north--south road in the United States. US 1 is generally paralleled by Interstate 95 (I-95), though the former is significantly farther west (inland) between Jacksonville, Florida, and Petersburg, Virginia. The highway connects most of the major cities of the East Coast--including Miami, Jacksonville, Richmond, Washington, D.C., Baltimore, Philadelphia, New York City and Boston, passing from the Southeastern United States to New England.
[0.590, 0.171] Original true response:  According to the knowledge provided, U.S. Route 1 starts at the Canada-US border in Fort Kent, Maine, and ends in Key West, Florida.

In New Brunswick, Canada, the short Route 161 extends north onthe New Brunswick side of the bridge to Route 120, a secondary East-West route from Edmundston, New Brunswick, west to Quebec Route 289 toward Saint-Alexandre, Quebec.
[0.775, 0.439] Prifix true response: U.S. Route 1 (US 1) is a major north--south U.S. Highway that spans 2,369 miles (3,815 km) from Fort Kent, Maine, at the Canadian border (on the International Boundary), to Key West, Florida, at the Atlantic Ocean. It is the longest numbered highway in the United States, and the most eastern, at least from the Canadian border. It is usually considered to be the longest north--south road in the United States, although, in Maine, it is co-aligned with Interstate 95 (I-95) for most of its length. From its northern terminus in Fort Kent, US 1 generally follows the coast of Maine, passing through Bangor, Portland, South Portland, Kennebunkport, and York, before entering New Hampshire.
[0.584, 0.176] Prifix ret response: US 1, from Maine to Key West, covers 3,113 miles, passing through 15 states and 43 counties in total, and from the Ocean to the Gulf of Mexico in some segments. In 1926 the U.S. Numbered Highway System was established to help tourists navigate from state to state, so the southern terminus of US 1 was officially designated in 1927 at Key West, Florida, where it ended at White Street (present-day Southard Avenue) at the Atlantic Ocean. The northern terminus of US 1 was established in 1933 at Fort Kent, Maine, where the highway meets the Canadian border.

Gas plants can ramp up to meet demand, making gas ideal for meeting peak electricity demand.

first jnanpith award was an autor of which language [/INST]
Ground truth: G. Sankara Kurup, (3 June 1901, Nayathode, Kingdom of Cochin (now in Ernakulam district, Kerala, India)--2 February 1978, Vappalassery, Angamaly, Ernakulam district, Kerala), better known as Mahakavi G (The Great Poet G), was the first winner of the Jnanpith Award, India's highest literary award. He won the prize in 1965 for his collection of poems in Malayalam Odakkuzhal (The Bamboo Flute, 1950). With part of the prize money he established the literary award Odakkuzhal in 1968. He was also the recipient of the Soviet Land Nehru Award, in 1967, and the Padma Bhushan in 1968. His poetry collection Viswadarshanam won the Kerala Sahitya Akademi Award in 1961 and Kendra Sahitya Akademi Award in 1963.
[0.631, 0.335] Original true response:  According to the provided knowledge, G. Sankara Kurup, also known as Mahakavi G, was the first winner of the Jnana Pith Award in 1965 forhis collection of poems in Malayalm, Odakkuzhal (TheBamboo Flute, 01950). Therefore, the author of the first Jnanpith Award is G. Sankara Kurup and the language is Malayalam.
[0.463, 0.104] Original ret response:  According to the knowledge provided, the first Jnanpith Award winner was a Malayalam author, so the answer is "Malayalam".
[0.608, 0.234] Prifix true response: K.V. Srinivasa Iyengar, (1904--81), first recipient of the Jnanpith Award, in 1965, for his epic in Kannada, Ugadi, now in English as The Morning of Creation (1971). He is known as the pride of Kannada, and is regarded as the second poet after Gurajada Apparao (1866--1927). His poem, Ugadi, is the longest poem in Kannada. He was awarded the Padma Bhushan in 1969. His works were translated into 17 languages, the latest being into the Russian language in 2006. His other notable works are Abhimanavva, Madhura Mantapa, Ragapraguna, Ragakumar, and Gitanjali. He was awarded the Magsaysay Award in 1963. His works are considered to be the extension of Vedic and Upanishadic wisdom and are considered as the glory of Kannada.
[0.542, 0.240] Prifix ret response: Sahitya Akademi Award, India's highest literary award, recognizes literary works in 24 languages. The recipients of the Jnanpith Award (the Jnanpith Award, India's highest literary honor) are selected in 13 languages, including Assamese, Bengali, Bodo, Dogri, Gujarati, Hindi, Kannada, Kashmiri, Marathi, Oriya, Punjabi, Sindhi, Tamil and Telugu. The Sahitya Akademi Award, in turn, honors works in 23 languages, excepting Sanskrit. The Jnanpith Award also recognizes literary contributions in English, while the Sahitya Akademi Award also recognizes works in Urdu. Chandidas, Tagore, Gyanendra, and Vyas are recipients of the Jnanpeth Award.

Ground truth: Cadbury, formerly Cadbury's, is a British multinational confectionery company wholly owned by Mondelez International (originally Kraft Foods) since 2010. It is the second-largest confectionery brand in the world after Wrigley's. Cadbury is internationally headquartered in Uxbridge, West London, and operates in more than 50 countries worldwide. One of the best-known British brands, in 2013 The Daily Telegraph named Cadbury among Britain's most successful exports.

where is nathan's hotdog eating contest held [/INST]
Ground truth: The Nathan's Hot Dog Eating Contest is an annual American hot dog competitive eating competition. It is held each year on Independence Day at Nathan's Famous Corporation's original, and best-known restaurant at the corner of Surf and Stillwell Avenues in Coney Island, a neighborhood of Brooklyn, New York City.

The contest is held at Coney Island's Steeplechase Park, now owned by the city of New York and operated by the New York City Parks and Recreation Department, and is broadcast live on ESPN, ESPN2, and ESPNews. It is one of the oldest, most prestigious, and most well-known competitive eating events in the United States.

Ground truth: My Country My Life is an autobiographical book by L.K. Advani, an Indian politician who served as the Deputy Prime Minister of India from 2002 to 2004, and was the Leader of the Opposition in the 15th Lok Sabha. The book was released on 19 March 2008 by Abdul Kalam, the eleventh President of India. The book has 1,040 pages and narrates autobiographical accounts and events in the life of Advani. It became the best seller book in the non-fiction category and Advani joined Archer as a bestseller author. The book website claims the book sold an excess of 1,000,000 copies. The book alongside mentions the event in Indian politics and India's history from 1900 till date.

The book was published in 2000 by HarperCollins and was the result of Suu Kyi's decision to give the 1995 Jonas Salk Award lecture, in which she chose to present an account of her life and the struggle of the Burmese people. The lecture was broadcast on CNN and the written transcript became the book. It was the first of Suu Kyi's books to be published in the United States and United Kingdom. The book became a New York Times Best Seller. The 2011 version has an introduction by actor Michael Palin. what does it mean to be on parole [/INST]
Ground truth: Parole is a temporary release of a prisoner who agrees to certain conditions before the completion of the maximum sentence period, originating from the French parole ("voice, spoken words"). The term became associated during the Middle Ages with the release of prisoners who gave their word.
[0.738, 0.456] Original true response:  Based on the knowledge provided, being on parole means being a temporary release of a prisoner who has agreed to certain conditions before the completion of their maximum sentence period. Parole is a type of early release program that allows prisoners to serve the remainder of their sentence in the community under supervision, rather than in prison. The conditions of parole may include regular meetings with a parole officer, employment or education requirements, and restrictions on activities such as travel or association with certain individuals. The term "parole" originated from the French word "parole," meaning "voice, spoken words," and became associated during the Middle Ageswith the release of prisoners who gave theirword.
[0.513, 0.155] Original ret response:  According to the knowledge provided, being on parole means that an individual who has been convicted of a crime and sentenced to serve time in a federal correctional facility has the opportunity to be released from prison early, under certain conditions. Parole is not guaranteed, and the length of parole can vary depending on the type of crime committed and the sentence imposed.

In general, parole is a type of early release from prison that allows an individual to serve the remainder of their sentence in the community under supervision. During this time, the individual is required to follow certain rules and conditions, such as reporting regularly to a parole officer, maintaining employment or attending school, and avoiding contact with certain people or places.

In the case of federal offenses, parole is typically available for individuals convicted of crimes other than first-degree murder. For first-degree murder convictions, an individual may be eligible for parole after serving at least 25 years of their sentence, provided they have been deemed to be a model prisoner and have shown that they are unlikely to commit further crimes. However, in cases where the individual has been convicted of multiple murders, the

The conditions imposed upon the parolee become the conditions of freedom during the period of supervision.

the origins of the stations of the cross [/INST]
Ground truth: The Stations of the Cross or the Way of the Cross, also known as the Way of Sorrows or the Via Crucis, refers to a series of images depicting Jesus Christ on the day of his crucifixion and accompanying prayers. The stations grew out of imitations of Via Dolorosa in Jerusalem which is believed to be the actual path Jesus walked to Mount Calvary. The object of the stations is to help the Christians faithful to make a spiritual pilgrimage through contemplation of the Passion of Christ. It has become one of the most popular devotions and the stations can be found in many Western Christian churches, including Anglican, Lutheran, Methodist, and Roman Catholic ones.
[0.694, 0.390] Original true response:  According to the knowledge provided, the origins of the Stations of the Cross or Way of the Cross can be traced back to imitations of the Via Dolorosa in Jerusalem, which is believed to be the actual Path Jesus walked to Mount Calvary on the day of his crucification.
[0.642, 0.289] Original ret response:  According to tradition, the Stations of the Cross, also known as the Way of the Cross or the Via Crucis, originated in the early Christian Church as a way for pilgrims to retrace the steps of Jesus Christ's passion and death. The exact origin of the Stations is unclear, but they are believed to have been established in the 4th century by the priest and theologian St. Ambrose in Milan, Italy.

The Stations of the Cross are a series of 14 stops or stations, each representing a significant event in Jesus' journey to his crucifixion. The stations are typically marked with a plaque or a statue, and pilgrims walk from one station to the next, reflecting on the event or scene depicted at each stop.

The Stations ofthe Cross became popular among pilgrims visiting the Holy Land in the Middle Ages, and they were often depicted in art and literature. Today, the Stations of the Cross are a common feature in many churches and are observed by millions of people around the world during Lent and other times of spiritual reflection.

[0.476, 0.112] Prifix ret response: Cooper Creek and Salt Creek drain water from Lake Eyre, supplying Lake Eyre with water when inflow from Georgina and Pandrup Creeks is low, or during the dry season, or in wet years. Dry years with high evaporation may cause the lake to be dry. The lake's surface area can change significantly depending on the time of year, from 4,000 square miles (10,000 km) in the wet season to less than 1 square mile (2.5 km) in the dry season. In addition, Lake Eyre is connected to the Gulf of Carpentaria via the Georgina River, which carries water from the lake to the Gulf.

who did the dominican republic gain its independence from [/INST]
Ground truth: The Dominican Independence War gave the Dominican Republic autonomy from Haiti on February 27, 1844. Before the war, the island of Hispaniola had been united under the Haitian government for a period of 22 years when the newly independent nation, then known as the Republic of Spanish Haiti, was invaded by Haiti in 1822. Previously known as the Captaincy General of Santo Domingo, the criollo class within the country overthrew the Spanish crown in 1821 before unifying with Haiti a year later.

Before the war, the island of Hispanoamerica had been under Spanish control for five centuries when the country known as the Captaincy General of Santo Domingo was created. When the Spanish Crown unilaterally abolished the captaincy system in 1821, the country became known as the Kingdom of Spain before being united with the rest of the Spanish Empire as the Kingdom of Spain in 1824.

who won the champions league final in 2016 [/INST]
Ground truth: The 2016 UEFA Champions League Final was the final match of the 2015--16 UEFA Champions League, the 61st season of Europe's premier club football tournament organised by UEFA, and the 24th season since it was renamed from the European Champion Clubs' Cup to the UEFA Champions League. It was played at the San Siro stadium in Milan, Italy, on 28 May 2016, between Spanish teams Real Madrid and Atlético Madrid, in a repeat of the 2014 final. It was the second time in the tournament's history that both finalists were from the same city. Real Madrid won 5--3 on a penalty shoot-out after a 1--1 draw at the end of extra time, securing a record-extending 11th title in the competition.
[0.596, 0.289] Original true response:  According to the provided knowledge, Real Madrid won the 2016 UEFA Champions league final against Atlético Madrid. They won the match on a penalty shoot-out after the game ended 1-1 at the end of extra time, with a score of 5-3.

[0.800, 0.579] Prifix true response: The 2016 UEFA Champions League Final was the final match of the 2015--16 UEFA Champions League, the 61st season of Europe's premier club football tournament organised by UEFA, and the 24th season since it was renamed from the European Cup to the UEFA Champions League. It was played at the San Siro in Milan, Italy on 28 May 2016, between Spanish side FC Barcelona and Italian side Juventus. It was the first final to be played at the San Siro since 2003, and the first to be played in May since 1998. when does a limit of a function not exist [/INST]
Ground truth: Formal definitions, first devised in the early 19th century, are given below. Informally, a function f assigns an output f (x) to every input x. We say the function has a limit L at an input p: this means f (x) gets closer and closer to L as x moves closer and closer to p. More specifically, when f is applied to any input sufficiently close to p, the output value is forced arbitrarily close to L. On the other hand, if some inputs very close to p are taken to outputs that stay a fixed distance apart, we say the limit does not exist.
[0.669, 0.264] Original true response:  According to the formal definition provided, a limit of a function does not exist if some inputs very close to a given point (called the "point of interest") are taken to outputs that stay afixed distance apart. In other words, if the function does not approach a specific value as the input gets arbitrarily close to the point of interest, then the limit does not exist at that point.
[0.553, 0.177] Original ret response:  According to the knowledge provided, a limit of a function does not exist in certain situations, including:

1. As special cases limits on an interval: The limit of a function may not exist at a point if the function is not defined on the entire interval containing that point. For example, the limit of a function f(x) as x approaches a may not exist if f(x) is only defined for x > a or x < a.
2. Left-handed limits: The limit of a function as s approaches a from the left may not exist if the function is not defined on a half-open interval of the form (−∞,a) or (a,∞). For example, the limit of f(x) as x approaches 0 from the left may not exist if f(x)$ is only defined for x > 0.
3. Right-handed limits: The limitof a function as s approaches a fromthe right may not exist if the function isnot defined on a half-open intervalof the form (a,∞) or (−∞,a). For example, the limit off(x) as x approaches ∞ from the right may not exist if f(x

The antiderivative is a function f (x) and the derivative is f (x), the derivative is f (x), and the derivative is f (x). A derivative is the same as an antiderivative. The derivative is a function f (x) that has a derivative f (x), and the derivative f (x) is equal to f (x).
[0.606, 0.191] Prifix ret response: As an infinitesimal sequence approaches a point, as an example, the limit of the quotient of the distance (L 1 (x, y)) from x to y (y, x) approaches a value of zero. In contrast, the limit of a function f (x) as x approaches a, (l, m) does not exist if there exists a ε > 0, so (x - l) > ε. A function can also have a non-existent limit (i.e., l ≠ f (l)) if f (x) has a discontinuity at l (l, m). what does the sun represent on the uruguay flag [/INST]
Ground truth: The horizontal stripes on the flag represent the nine original departments of Uruguay, based on the U.S flag, where the stripes represent the original 13 colonies. The first flag designed in 1828 had 9 light blue stripes; this number was reduced to 4 in 1830 due to visibility problems from distance. The Sun of May represents the May Revolution of 1810; according to the historian Diego Abad de Santillán, the Sun of May is a figurative sun that represents Inti, the sun god of the Inca religion. It also appears in the Flag of Argentina and the Coat of Arms of Bolivia.
[0.718, 0.424] Original true response:  According to the knowledge provided, the Sun of May on the Uruguay flag represents the May Revolution of 1780. The Sun of May is a figurative representation of the sun god Inti from the Inca religion, and it also appears on the flags of Argentina and Bolivia.
[0.586, 0.182] Original ret response:  According to the knowledge provided, the sun on the Uruguayan flag represents the May Revolution of 1810.
[0.600, 0.265] Prifix true response: The flag of Uruguay covers the national coat of arms on a blue field, based on the flag of the 1825 Constitución Política, which in turn uses the flag of the Spanish Navy. The national coat of arms displays a golden sun on blue field; the emblem also includes a shield with the national motto, ¡Tierra y Libertad, also in gold. The sun also appears on the coat of arms of the president of Uruguay, and is also the basis for the flag of the town of Montevideo. According to a tradition based on the flag of the First National Government of Uruguay, the color blue is also used to represent the color of the sky and the Atlantic Ocean.
[0.550, 0.275] Prifix ret response: Flag of Uruguay features green. red and white horizontal stripes. Green represents the country’s fertility and the richness of the soil; the red represents the country’s national spirit and the blood of the national heroes, while the white represents the purity of the country. This flag was officially adopted on May 25, 1828; this was to honor the independence of the country from Spain. According to the country’s laws, the flag cannot be altered; this is to protect the flag from any form of modification that might cause a conflict with the nation’s sovereignty. The flag is to be raised on the official day of the flag on May 25.

when did beryl markham fly across the atlantic [/INST]
Ground truth: When Markham decided to take on the Atlantic crossing, no female pilot had yet flown non-stop from Europe to New York, and no woman had made the westward flight solo, though several had died trying. Markham hoped to claim both records. On 4 September 1936, she took off from Abingdon, England. After a 20-hour flight, her Vega Gull, The Messenger, suffered fuel starvation due to icing of the fuel tank vents, and she crash-landed at Baleine Cove on Cape Breton Island, Nova Scotia, Canada. She became the first person to make it from England to North America non-stop from east to west. She was celebrated as an aviation pioneer.
[0.598, 0.265] Original true response:  According to the knowledge provided, Beryl Markham flew across the Atlantic Ocean on September 4, 1936. She took off from Abingdon,England and landed at Baleine Cove, Nova Scotia, Canada after a 20-hour flight. Therefore, the answer to the question "When did Beryl Markham fly across the Atlantic?" is: September 4, 1926.
[0.563, 0.127] Original ret response:  According to the knowledge provided, Beryl Markham successfully flew across the Atlantic from east to west non-stop in 1930, making her the first woman to accomplish this feat.
[0.601, 0.217] Prifix true response: When she finally took to the air on 1 September 1936, Beryl Markham had had enough of being snubbed by the men who had had all the glory of flight, and she wanted to be the first woman to cross the Atlantic solo. She had made several failed attempts on an open-copped biplane she had designed herself, and, after the failure of her fourth attempt, she rebuilt her aircraft from a fabric-covered steel tube. Her first flight across the Atlantic took 18 hours, 45 minutes, and landed her in the harbour at Canso, Nova Scotia. She became an instant celebrity and her name was known across the world. After that she set several other records.
[0.591, 0.173] Prifix ret response: Because of her experiences as a child on a colonial farm, Markham was invited to join a group of wealthy women on a plane. On 19 May 1936, she became the first woman to cross the Atlantic east to west, some 28 days before Amelia Earhart. She made the flight solo in her de Havilland Gypsy Moth plane, The Berylmark (named after herself). The weather was bad for most of the flight, making it the most challenging part of her journey. She flew for over 20 hours a day, from Darlington, England to Toronto, Canada.

when did under the cork tree come out [/INST]
Ground truth: From Under the Cork Tree is the second studio album by American rock band Fall Out Boy. It was released on May 3, 2005, through Island Records as the band's major label debut. The music was composed by lead vocalist and guitarist Patrick Stump, with all lyrics penned by bassist Pete Wentz, continuing the band's songwriting approach they took for some songs on their prior 2003 effort Take This to Your Grave. Neal Avron handled production duties. Commenting on the record's lyrical themes, Wentz said the lyrics were about "the anxiety and depression that goes along with looking at your own life." In support of their release the group headlined tours worldwide and played at various music festivals. For their Black Clouds and Underdogs tour the album was re-released as From Under the Cork Tree (Limited "Black Clouds and Underdogs" Edition), featuring new songs and remixes.

It was the last album under his MCA contract, and the label rejected the mix of the album, so DOOM took it to Bad Boy Records. where does eye of the round roast come from [/INST]
Ground truth: A round steak is a beef steak from the "round", the rear leg of the cow. The round is divided into cuts including the eye (of) round, bottom round, and top round, with or without the "round" bone (femur), and may include the knuckle (sirloin tip), depending on how the round is separated from the loin. This is a lean cut and it is moderately tough. Lack of fat and marbling makes round dry out when cooked with dry-heat cooking methods like roasting or grilling. Round steak is commonly prepared with slow moist-heat methods including braising, to tenderize the meat and maintain moisture. The cut is often sliced thin, then dried or smoked at low temperature to make jerky.
[0.556, 0.197] Original true response:  According to the knowledge provided, the eye of round roast comes from the "eye" cut of the round, which is a cut of beef from the rear leg of the cow.
[0.595, 0.278] Original ret response:  According to the knowledge provided, "eye of the round" is a cut of beef that comes from the round primal cut. Specifically, it is the most tender and flavorful part of the round, which is located near the hip of the cow. The eye of the round roast is a popular cut for roasting, as it is tender and juicy, and can be cooked to a variety of temperatures to suit different tastes.
[0.654, 0.327] Prifix true response: A round roast is a cut of beef from the primal "round". The round is the hindquarters of the cow, including the rump (rump roast), the loin (tenderloin and sirloin tip roast), and the round, including the eye of round (e.g., rump roast, eye of round roast). The eye of round is a very lean cut of meat. The lean cuts are on the bottom and the fattier cuts are on top. When cooking the eye of round roast, it is often cut into steaks or roasted and cooked. The eye of round is often less tender and thicker than tenderloin. When cooked to the right temperature, the meat is tender and juicy. Often the eye of round is less expensive than tenderloin.
[0.471, 0.147] Prifix ret response: The American English term "eye of the round" (e.g. "round roast with eyes" or "eye of round roast") can sometimes also occur in North American English (e.g. "eye of round roast with vegetables"). From 1800 to 1830, American and British English vocabularies were merged and influenced by other dialects from the Atlantic world, including Caribbean English and Native American English; these influences are found in American English words and phrases from the period, and continue to this day. From 1900 to 1920, American English experienced a period of Americanization (Americanization of British English), resulting in a significant number of words from American English becoming current in British English.

* In 1987, when the United States extended DST to the first Sunday in April, all DST-observing Canadian provinces followed suit.
* In 2005, when the United States made changes to its DST schedule under the Energy Policy Act of 2005, adding parts of March and November starting in 2007:
	+ Ontario: October 20, 2015
	+ Manitoba: October 20, 1965
	+ Quebec: December 5, 2007
	+ Prince Edward Island: December 6, 2007
 	+ New Brunswick: December 23, 2017

Therefore, the dates when Canada switches to Daylight Saving Time are:

* October 20, 186

<</SYS>>

where do bullets go when fired in the air [/INST]
Ground truth: Bullets fired into the air usually fall back with terminal velocities much lower than their muzzle velocity when they leave the barrel of a firearm. Nevertheless, people can be injured, sometimes fatally, when bullets discharged into the air fall back down to the ground. Bullets fired at angles less than vertical are more dangerous as the bullet maintains its angular ballistic trajectory and is far less likely to engage in tumbling motion; it therefore travels at speeds much higher than a bullet in free fall.
[0.712, 0.342] Original true response:  According to the knowledge provided, bullets fired into the air usually fallback with terminal velocities much lower than the muzzle velocity when they leave a firearm.
[0.776, 0.578] Original ret response:  According to the knowledge provided, bullets fired into the air usually falls back with terminal velocities much lower their muzzle velocity when they leaves the barrel of a firearm, but people can still be injured, sometimes fatally, by bullets discharged into the  air that fall back down to the ground.
[0.647, 0.243] Prifix true response: Bullets usually travel at much higher velocities than bullets fired at sea level when they are fired at high altitudes where the air pressure is lower. Bullets can travel at velocities up to 2,000 mph, however, the air pressure at sea level is significantly higher than at higher altitudes. When fired at higher altitudes the bullet is less likely to penetrate its target; therefore it is often considered more effective to use a bullet that is less likely to penetrate at lower altitudes than at sea level.
[0.627, 0.203] Prifix ret response: Bullets usually fall back to the ground unless they are spin stabilized to be less affected by gravity in flight so they can fly a longer distance. Bullets are fired at very high velocities, up to 300 mph, so when they fall back to the ground, they are simply moving too fast to be shot down by another weapon or slowed by friction. A bullet fired at a range of 100 yards is in the atmosphere for only 2.5 seconds and it takes the bullet 0.6 seconds to travel up to its peak altitude; the remaining 1.9 seconds is the time it takes to fall back to the ground.

what was the alliance between great britian russia and france known as [/INST]
Ground truth: The Triple Entente (from French entente (ɑ̃tɑ̃t) "friendship, understanding, agreement") refers to the understanding linking the Russian Empire, the French Third Republic, and the United Kingdom of Great Britain and Ireland after the signing of the Anglo-Russian Entente on 31 August 1907. The understanding between the three powers, supplemented by agreements with Japan and Portugal, was a powerful counterweight to the Triple Alliance of Germany, Austria-Hungary, and Italy.
[0.649, 0.165] Original true response:  The alliance between Great Britain, Russia, and France was known as the Triple Entente.
[0.586, 0.135] Original ret response:  Based on the knowledge provided, the alliance between Britain, Russia, and France was known as the Entente Cordiale.
[0.621, 0.331] Prifix true response: The Triple Entente (from the French entente (meaning "friendship", "understanding", "agreement") negotiated by the Russian Ambassador to France, Count Benoist de l’Averda, and the Russian naval attaché, Captain Andrei Scherbatoff. The Triple Entente signed a secret agreement on 3 August, the same day as the signing of the German-Russian agreement. After the signing of the agreement, a ceremony was held on the Russian battleship Admiral Kopenskij.
[0.542, 0.181] Prifix ret response: France and Britain formed an alliance, known as the Entente Cordiale, that resolved a series of colonial disputes in North Africa. The treaty was largely negotiated by British Foreign Secretary Sir Edward Grey in the spring of 1904 and was the work of several rounds of diplomatic talks between the two countries. As the agreement was to be kept secret, the negotiations were conducted in great secrecy, and the British government did not disclose the terms of the agreement to the French parliament until 1905. The terms were not disclosed to the British people until the Entente Cordiale was officially signed in Paris in January 1905.

when was the death penalty reinstated in oregon [/INST]
Ground truth: In 1984, Measure 6 amended the state constitution to once more make the death penalty legal. Measure 7, a statutory measure passed in the same year, required a separate sentencing hearing before a jury in cases of aggravated murder.

[0.567, 0.167] Prifix ret response: "Death penalty reinstated" was not banned in Oregon, and capital punishment remains legal in the state under certain circumstances. Since 1994, Oregon has been a death penalty free state, and the legislature has been inactive in reinstating the death penalty. However, with the exception of a temporary moratorium between 1994 and 1996, the death penalty was in place from 1990 to 1994. In 1994, voters approved Measure 16, an initiative repealing the death penalty. However, a moratorium on executions was lifted in 1996 by Governor John Kitzhaber, allowing capital punishment to be reinstated.

The client included features such as buddy lists, contact lists, file transfers, chat rooms, text messaging, and alerts. The Betwa originates in the south, near Bharatpur in Rajasthan, and meets the Yamuna in Agra.

when was the national flag of india adopted [/INST]
Ground truth: The National Flag of India is a horizontal rectangular tricolour of India saffron, white and India green; with the Ashoka Chakra, a 24-spoke wheel, in navy blue at its centre. It was adopted in its present form during a meeting of the Constituent Assembly held on 22 July 1947, and it became the official flag of the Dominion of India on 15 August 1947. The flag was subsequently retained as that of the Republic of India. In India, the term "tricolour" (Hindi: तिरंगा, translit. Tiraṅgā) almost always refers to the Indian national flag. The flag is based on the Swaraj flag, a flag of the Indian National Congress designed by Pingali Venkayya.
[0.600, 0.312] Original true response:  According to the knowledge provided, the National Flag of India was adopted in its present form during "a meeting of the Constituent Assembly" held on July 22, 1947.
[0.579, 0.187] Original ret response:  According to the knowledge provided, the national flag of India, also known as the Swaraj flag, was officially adopted by the Indian National Congress in 1931.
[0.775, 0.514] Prifix true response: The National Flag of India is a horizontal rectangular tricolour of India saffron, white and India green; with the Ashoka Chakra, a 24-spoke wheel, in navy blue at its centre. It was adopted in its present form on 22 July 1947, just before India became an independent nation. The flag was subsequently formalised by the Indian National Congress in 1949, though its basic design had been used by the Indian National Movement for almost a century. In India, it is manufactured by the Indian Printers and Stationers Corporation (IPSC), based at Delhi. The term "tricolour" (Hindi: तrikाओं, Trikāng) is Indian, as "tak Battalion".
[0.603, 0.277] Prifix ret response: An early flag of the country. It was sometimes blue. By the end of the century it was still in use, as the Indian flag became the basis of the flag of the East India Company and later the flag of the British Raj. The Indian National Congress chose the flag in 1921 as the flag of the Dominion of India, adopted as the national flag of India in 1947 by the Constituent Assembly of India, including a number of unofficial designs. The flag thus underwent a number of changes in design as the country emerged as an independent nation, becoming the basis of the flag of the Republic of India.

who is often associated with printing the first book using moveable type in germany [/INST]
Ground truth: Johannes Gutenberg of Mainz, Germany is acknowledged as the first to invent a metal movable-type printing system in Europe, the printing press. Gutenberg was a goldsmith familiar with techniques of cutting punches for making coins from moulds. Between 1436 and 1450 he developed hardware and techniques for casting letters from matrices using a device called the hand mould. Gutenberg's key invention and contribution to movable-type printing in Europe, the hand mould, was the first practical means of making cheap copies of letterpunches in the vast quantities needed to print complete books, making the movable-type printing process a viable enterprise.
[0.544, 0.130] Original true response:  According to the knowledge provided, Johannes Gutenberg is often associated with printing the first Book using moveable type in Germany.
[0.564, 0.153] Original ret response:  Johannes Gutenberg is often associated with printing the first books using moveable type in Germany.
[0.646, 0.250] Prifix true response: Johannes Gutenberg is, in effect, the first person to achieve a major breakthrough in printing technology, in the Western world. A German goldsmith by training, Gutenberg was the first to create a printing press using movable type. His inventions in type metal casting, casting for printing, and ink were revolutionary in achieving cheaper books and more consistent output. Gutenberg's achievements were made possible by the introduction of a new ink, the adaptation of an existing wine press to print in both directions, and the design of movable type pieces cast in lead. With Gutenberg's printing-press innovations, all Western literature from the Renaissance through the Enlightenment was printed from moveable type.
[0.596, 0.191] Prifix ret response: To illustrate the moveable-type printing process, woodcut blocks were glued together on a wooden frame. By the mid-15th century, a few moveable-type printing presses were being built and used; a very influential example was the invention of the first moveable-type printing press by Johannes Gutenberg, a German goldsmith and printer. Gutenberg was born around 1400 and grew up in Mainz; his innovations and wealth made him a very important person to both the church and the political leaders of the time.

Ground truth: The Battle of Stirling Bridge was a battle of the First War of Scottish Independence. On 11 September 1297, the forces of Andrew Moray and William Wallace defeated the combined English forces of John de Warenne, 6th Earl of Surrey, and Hugh de Cressingham near Stirling, on the River Forth.

Prompt: what do the 3 dots mean in math
Ground truth: In logical argument and mathematical proof, the therefore sign (∴) is generally used before a logical consequence, such as the conclusion of a syllogism. The symbol consists of three dots placed in an upright triangle and is read therefore. It is encoded at U + 2234 ∴ therefore (HTML & # 8756; &there4;). For common use in Microsoft Office hold the ALT key and type "8756". While it is not generally used in formal writing, it is used in mathematics and shorthand. It is complementary to U + 2235 ∵ because (HTML & # 8757;).
[0.516, 0.171] Original true response: In addition to also meaning "you know what I mean", the series also uses the 3 dots in elementary math to indicate functions, but only very rarely use them in calculus; because the number of functions in this series is so large, it is hard to get a handle on how they are defined. It is said that the three dots are simply there to represent the author's failure to write down all the functions in the book. In summary, the three dots (Ω) in mathematics (and other fields) serve a variety of purposes. The use of a three-dot symbol is not widespread. It is not part of a common mathematical notation.

Prompt: who was the first chief minister of west bengal
Ground truth: Since 1947, there have been eight Chief Ministers of West Bengal. The first was Prafulla Chandra Ghosh of the Indian National Congress, who was succeeded by his party-mates Dr Bidhan Chandra Roy and Prafulla Chandra Sen. A decade of instability followed, marred by fractious coalition governments and frequent impositions of President's rule. The instability ended with 1977 election victory of the Communist Party of India (Marxist) (CPM). Headed by Jyoti Basu, the CPM-led Left Front government was in office for over 23 years (1977--2000). Left rule in West Bengal continued for another 10 years under Buddhadeb Bhattacharya, before its defeat in the 2011 election by the Trinamool Congress. Appointed on 20 May 2011, Trinamool leader Mamata Banerjee is the current incumbent, the state's first woman chief minister.
[0.557, 0.187] Original true response: 8 August 1947, Jawaharlal Nehru, India's first Prime Minister, announced the Constitution of India, India's first Chief Minister, Dr. B.C. Roy, is credited with being the first Chief Minister of West Bengal. As the Governor-General's Executive Council's member for Bengal, Roy served under Governor-General Louis Mountbatten. On 21 August 1947, the Governor-General appointed him the first Chief Minister of West Bengali. In the 1947 Bengali Provincial Assembly election, the Indian National Congress won 246 seats out of 258, and on 24 August, the same day of the election, Roy took oath as the Chief Minister.

Prompt: what is the maximum data rate for the 802.11a standard select one
Ground truth: The 802.11 a standard uses the same data link layer protocol and frame format as the original standard, but an OFDM based air interface (physical layer). It operates in the 5 GHz band with a maximum net data rate of 54 Mbit/s, plus error correction code, which yields realistic net achievable throughput in the mid-20 Mbit/s.
[0.609, 0.193] Original true response: In IEEE 802.11 and 802.11b /g /n /ac /ad /af /ah standards, transmission rates have been upgraded. The maximum data rate for the standard is 54 Mbps. The standard also allows for 108 Mbps and 600 Mbps speeds. While the standard supports 600 Mbps, it uses multiple antennas to increase speed, but only 187 Mbps can be achieved at a single antenna. The standard allows for the use of the 5 GHz band, but it only operates in the 2.4 GHz band.

Prompt: where does the term dog and pony show come from
Ground truth: The term was originally used in the United States in the late-19th and early-20th centuries to refer to small traveling circuses that toured through small towns and rural areas. The name derives from the common use of performing dogs and ponies as the main attractions of the events. Performances were generally held in open-air arenas, such as race tracks or public spaces in localities that were too small or remote to attract larger, more elaborate performers or performances. The most notorious was "Prof. Gentry's Famous Dog & Pony Show," started when teenager Henry Gentry and his brothers started touring in 1886 with their act, originally entitled "Gentry's Equine and Canine Paradox." It started small, but evolved into a full circus show. Other early dog and pony shows included Morris' Equine and Canine Paradoxes (1883) and Hurlburt's Dog and Pony Show (late 1880s).
[0.501, 0.139] Original true response: From the start of the show--these dogs are bred to be very "pony-like". They are actually called a "Basset Hound". This breed is "pony-like" because they are so short and are usually dark. So they will be able to stand on the pony show grounds with the ponies and not be any taller than the ponies. These dogs are bred to be small and to be taller than normal dogs. This is the reason they are called pony-like. The name pony is used because of this breed. They are small and pony-like. They are about the same height as ponies and look like ponies. This is why the show is called the pony show. So this is where the term dog and pony show comes from. This term is used to describe any kind of show.

Prompt: who is recognized as the founder of islam
Ground truth: According to tradition, the Islamic prophet Muhammad was born in Mecca around the year 570. His family belonged to the Quraysh. When he was about forty years old, he began receiving what Muslims consider to be divine revelations delivered through the angel Gabriel, which would later form the Quran, enjoining him to proclaim a strict monotheistic faith, warn his compatriots of the impending Judgement Day, and castigate social injustices of his city. Muhammad's message won over a handful of followers and was met with increasing opposition from notables of Mecca. In 618, after he lost protection with the death of his influential uncle Abu Talib, Muhammad took flight to the city of Yathrib (subsequently called Medina) where he was joined by his followers. Later generations would count this event, known as the hijra, as the start of the Islamic era.

His father, Abdullah, had been adopted by a prominent Meccan clan, the Banu Hashim, and thus Muhammad's mother Aminah was descended from their clan. His marriage to Khadija, a wealthy and successful businesswoman, marked the beginning of his prophetic mission (which would eventually lead to the formation of the Islamic faith).

Prompt: how long is training camp for nfl players
Ground truth: In the National Football League, training camp refers to the time before the season commences. During this time, teams will sometimes congregate at an outside location, usually a university, to conduct training camp for at least the first few weeks. This is similar to baseball's spring training.
[0.529, 0.143] Original true response: Camp lengths. All camps are full-time, and the length of each is regulated. NFL training camps are typically divided into two phases. Phase one includes on-field workouts, and Phase two includes two preseason games, with NFL teams restricted to eighty-nine players at the start of the second phase. The length of training camp varies with the length of the regular season, from three to five weeks. Teams have the option to start training camp one week before the start of the season and end training camp three days before the start of the season. Teams are allowed to hold five preseason games and must reduce the roster to 53 players by the end of the fourth game.

Prompt: who breaks a tie in the us senate
Ground truth: The Vice President of the United States is the ex officio President of the Senate, as provided in Article I, Section 3, Clause 4, of the United States Constitution, but may only vote in order to break a tie. According to the U.S. Senate, as of February 28, 2018, a tie-breaking vote had been cast 264 times by 36 vice presidents.
[0.667, 0.348] Original true response: By order of precedence rules adopted by the Senate in 1789 the president of the Senate is required to vote in the case of a tie in the election of the Vice President of the United States (Article I Section 3 of the United States Constitution). This rule is listed in the Senate Manual as well as in the Congressional Record (150 Congressional Record 2543). As of  2014, there have been 154 instances where the Vice President has been required to cast a tie-breaking vote in the Senate (Congressional Record 154).

Prompt: where does us highway 1 start and end
Ground truth: U.S. Route 1 (US 1) is a major north--south U.S. Highway that serves the East Coast of the United States. It runs 2,369 miles (3,813 km), from Fort Kent, Maine, at the Canada--US border, south to Key West, Florida, making it the longest north--south road in the United States. US 1 is generally paralleled by Interstate 95 (I-95), though the former is significantly farther west (inland) between Jacksonville, Florida, and Petersburg, Virginia. The highway connects most of the major cities of the East Coast--including Miami, Jacksonville, Richmond, Washington, D.C., Baltimore, Philadelphia, New York City and Boston, passing from the Southeastern United States to New England.
[0.688, 0.305] Original true response: US Highway 1, officially the Lincoln Highway, is the east coast alternative to the Interstate Highway System. The Lincoln Highway is the east coast of the United States. US 1 is the longest U.S. highway, stretching 2,369 miles from Savannah, Georgia (US 17) to Key West, Florida (US  1), although parts of the road are now located along interstate highways, including Interstate 95. The route generally follows the coastline of the Atlantic Ocean, although parts of the road are located inland, mostly in the Delaware Valley. US 1 continues through New England, passing through the New York City borough of Queens, Connecticut, Rhode Island, and Massachusetts.

Sankara Kurup, (3 June 1901, Nayathode, Kingdom of Cochin (now in Ernakulam district, Kerala, India)--2 February 1978, Vappalassery, Angamaly, Ernakulam district, Kerala), better known as Mahakavi G (The Great Poet G), was the first winner of the Jnanpith Award, India's highest literary award. He won the prize in 1965 for his collection of poems in Malayalam Odakkuzhal (The Bamboo Flute, 1950). With part of the prize money he established the literary award Odakkuzhal in 1968. He was also the recipient of the Soviet Land Nehru Award, in 1967, and the Padma Bhushan in 1968. His poetry collection Viswadarshanam won the Kerala Sahitya Akademi Award in 1961 and Kendra Sahitya Akademi Award in 1963.
[0.593, 0.249] Original true response: The Jnanpith Award, India's highest literary award in the Bengali language. It recognises literary works in the Indian languages. The Jnanpith Award selection committee (JNAP) awarded it in 1965 to the Kakababu (Kolkata, 1962) of Upendrakishore Roychowdhury. In 1977, he also received the Padma Bhushan award. His notable works include Kakababu, Baba Pratap, Ajana Adhunika and others. In 1977 he received the Padma Bhushan, India's third highest civilian award, and in 1982 he received the Padma Vibhushan, India's second highest civilian award.

Prompt: the origins of the stations of the cross
Ground truth: The Stations of the Cross or the Way of the Cross, also known as the Way of Sorrows or the Via Crucis, refers to a series of images depicting Jesus Christ on the day of his crucifixion and accompanying prayers. The stations grew out of imitations of Via Dolorosa in Jerusalem which is believed to be the actual path Jesus walked to Mount Calvary. The object of the stations is to help the Christians faithful to make a spiritual pilgrimage through contemplation of the Passion of Christ. It has become one of the most popular devotions and the stations can be found in many Western Christian churches, including Anglican, Lutheran, Methodist, and Roman Catholic ones.
[0.632, 0.323] Original true response: The Stations of the Cross are a Christian devotion commemorating the Passion of Jesus Christ as described in the New Testament of the Bible at the site of his crucifixion: Calvary is the location of the Via Dolorosa in Jerusalem, although not necessarily the actual path of the events depicted in the stations. In addition to the Via Dolorosa in Jerusalem (though not necessarily the exact path of the events depicted in a particular station), the stations of the cross are often located in a church, churchyard, or other sacred place, along a path or trail leading to a site of pilgrimage.

Prompt: what does it mean to be on parole
Ground truth: Parole is a temporary release of a prisoner who agrees to certain conditions before the completion of the maximum sentence period, originating from the French parole ("voice, spoken words"). The term became associated during the Middle Ages with the release of prisoners who gave their word.
[0.486, 0.097] Original true response: That certain offenders must abide by their parole officer or agency for certain time periods after their prison sentences are reduced or expired. Violations of parole can result in re-incarceration. Parole is not mandatory for all offenders, however. Most states have the option of imposing parole supervision. If imposed, parole supervision can be either mandatory or discretionary, depending on the state or jurisdiction. In mandatory supervision states (34 states), offenders have no right to refuse parole supervision. In discretionary supervision states (26 states), offenders have a right to refuse parole supervision if they have completed their sentence.

Prompt: when does a limit of a function not exist
Ground truth: Formal definitions, first devised in the early 19th century, are given below. Informally, a function f assigns an output f (x) to every input x. We say the function has a limit L at an input p: this means f (x) gets closer and closer to L as x moves closer and closer to p. More specifically, when f is applied to any input sufficiently close to p, the output value is forced arbitrarily close to L. On the other hand, if some inputs very close to p are taken to outputs that stay a fixed distance apart, we say the limit does not exist.
[0.543, 0.127] Original true response: As an infinite series as an example may be divergent, a series as an infinite sum of terms by an infinite geometric series (i.e., an infinite sum of terms, each of which is obtained by multiplying the previous one by a fixed non-zero number). It may be shown to be divergent by taking the limit of the partial sums of the series as the partial sum increases, which does not approach any finite value. In other words, no finite limit exists in the series. In the same way, a limit of a function may not exist if the function does not converge (i.e., the function does not approach a finite value as the variable goes to infinity). In particular, the limit of a function does not exist if the function has a pole (i.e., a point at which the function goes to infinity).

Prompt: what does the sun represent on the uruguay flag
Ground truth: The horizontal stripes on the flag represent the nine original departments of Uruguay, based on the U.S flag, where the stripes represent the original 13 colonies. The first flag designed in 1828 had 9 light blue stripes; this number was reduced to 4 in 1830 due to visibility problems from distance. The Sun of May represents the May Revolution of 1810; according to the historian Diego Abad de Santillán, the Sun of May is a figurative sun that represents Inti, the sun god of the Inca religion. It also appears in the Flag of Argentina and the Coat of Arms of Bolivia.
[0.594, 0.230] Original true response: According to the Uruguayan Constitution of 1997; the red and white stripes were adopted from the flag of Buenos Aires, which was under Spanish influence. The adoption of a red and white striped flag was a deliberate rejection of the influence of the Spanish flag, the red and white flag of Castile, which was associated with Spanish colonialism. The white and red colors were also used by the Celtiberian and Celtic tribes. The sun was first introduced on the national flag by President Félix Óptimo Muttoni on 25 August 1828. The use of the sun was due to the ideal of the sun representing the vital and active principles of the country.

Prompt: when did beryl markham fly across the atlantic
Ground truth: When Markham decided to take on the Atlantic crossing, no female pilot had yet flown non-stop from Europe to New York, and no woman had made the westward flight solo, though several had died trying. Markham hoped to claim both records. On 4 September 1936, she took off from Abingdon, England. After a 20-hour flight, her Vega Gull, The Messenger, suffered fuel starvation due to icing of the fuel tank vents, and she crash-landed at Baleine Cove on Cape Breton Island, Nova Scotia, Canada. She became the first person to make it from England to North America non-stop from east to west. She was celebrated as an aviation pioneer.
[0.580, 0.149] Original true response: Because of a severe storm on her first attempt, Beryl Markham and a crew of two made a good on the island of Ascension. They continued on to a base in the Azores, where Markham took over a smaller plane, finishing her solo journey with only a few hours to spare. Markham became the first woman to make the Atlantic crossing on her own. The flight took 19 days (1936). Markham flew a Percival Vega Gull monoplane, a sturdy two-seat biplane; a year later, she set a record for the highest altitude flown by a woman in a powered aircraft (23,687 feet). In 1947, she attempted to fly solo from the United Kingdom to Australia, making the first leg of the flight in a Percival Provence.

Ground truth: United States Declaration of Independence 1823 facsimile of the engrossed copy Created June--July 1776 Ratified July 4, 1776 Location Engrossed copy: National Archives Rough draft: Library of Congress Author (s) Thomas Jefferson et al. (engrosser: probably Timothy Matlack) Signatories 56 delegates to the Continental Congress Purpose To announce and explain separation from Great Britain
[0.454, 0.124] Original true response: Two men wrote a first draft of the Declaration. Thomas Jefferson wrote most of the Declaration of Independence. A leading advocate of independence. A wealthy landowner in Virginia, Jefferson wrote the most of the Declaration, which was then edited by a committee of five men. One of whom, John Adams of Massachusetts, took a leading role in its presentation to Congress and was given the task of writing the formal announcement of independence to the people. While Jefferson is credited with the main body of the Declaration, there were earlier and widespread influences; some of his own pre-existing drafts were largely drawn from earlier models, while some of his writing was inspired by similarities to the British works of John Locke.

Prompt: where does eye of the round roast come from
Ground truth: A round steak is a beef steak from the "round", the rear leg of the cow. The round is divided into cuts including the eye (of) round, bottom round, and top round, with or without the "round" bone (femur), and may include the knuckle (sirloin tip), depending on how the round is separated from the loin. This is a lean cut and it is moderately tough. Lack of fat and marbling makes round dry out when cooked with dry-heat cooking methods like roasting or grilling. Round steak is commonly prepared with slow moist-heat methods including braising, to tenderize the meat and maintain moisture. The cut is often sliced thin, then dried or smoked at low temperature to make jerky.
[0.593, 0.258] Original true response: A round roast is a cut of beef from the rear quarter, including the eye muscle. The "eye" is the darker, fattier eye muscle (the most tender part), and is a popular roast-cut (when cooked with the bone on, the cut is more tender), particularly for family gatherings and special occasions: holidays, dinner parties, barbecues, and camping trips- this is where the term ` eye of the round roast ` comes from. This beef roast is also occasionally used for the dish ` roast beef with roasted vegetables ` (usually potatoes, carrots, onions, and sometimes cabbage or Brussels sprouts).

Prompt: when does canada switch to daylight savings time
Ground truth: In regions where daylight saving time is used, it commences on the second Sunday of March, and standard time restarts on the first Sunday in November.
[0.583, 0.164] Original true response: Daylight saving time in Canada applies in most time zones, with most clocks springing forward (eastward) one hour (24 hours) in the last week of March (North American Daylight Time) or most clocks falling back one hour (24 hours) the last Sunday in October. However, most of the Yukon and parts of Quebec (including the islands of the St. Lawrence River and the surrounding area, though the rest of Quebec observes standard time year-round) do not observe daylight saving time. The provinces of Saskatchewan and most of Alberta observe standard time all year round, but those in the Mountain Time Zone do observe the daylight saving schedule.

Prompt: where do bullets go when fired in the air
Ground truth: Bullets fired into the air usually fall back with terminal velocities much lower than their muzzle velocity when they leave the barrel of a firearm. Nevertheless, people can be injured, sometimes fatally, when bullets discharged into the air fall back down to the ground. Bullets fired at angles less than vertical are more dangerous as the bullet maintains its angular ballistic trajectory and is far less likely to engage in tumbling motion; it therefore travels at speeds much higher than a bullet in free fall.
[0.639, 0.236] Original true response: Bullets usually leave the barrel of the gun at the same velocity they leave the muzzle of the gun when they are fired, so the bullet can go any direction. In the case of a bullet fired in air, it will be deflected back to the ground, unless it is fired at a high enough angle of entry and therefore a trajectory is calculated to be at a lower angle than the angle of impact; the bullet may penetrate a few feet into the ground at a higher angle of entry and is often effective in attacking targets behind barriers. The exact behavior of a bullet in flight is determined by the shape and size of the bullet and its trajectory and the spin imparted upon it.

Prompt: what was icq as related to instant messaging
Ground truth: ICQ is an instant messaging client that was first developed and popularized by the Israeli company Mirabilis in 1996. The name ICQ derives from the English phrase "I Seek You". Ownership of ICQ passed from Mirabilis to AOL in 1998, and from AOL to Mail.Ru Group in 2010.
[0.486, 0.164] Original true response: ICQ saved much space through its use of delta encoding. Whenever two ICQ users exchanged messages (called "pinging"), ICQ (along with other instant messaging systems, see "MIME") used delta encoding. This allowed ICQ to store all messages in a compact binary format, and extract any new messages by comparing the delta between the current state and the previous state. The delta encoding was so efficient that ICQ could store a message in about 12 bytes. A similar approach to delta encoding was used in the Jabber instant messaging system (based upon a specification called the Extensible Messaging and Presence Protocol, or XMPP), to reduce the size of instant messages (IMs).

Prompt: when was the national flag of india adopted
Ground truth: The National Flag of India is a horizontal rectangular tricolour of India saffron, white and India green; with the Ashoka Chakra, a 24-spoke wheel, in navy blue at its centre. It was adopted in its present form during a meeting of the Constituent Assembly held on 22 July 1947, and it became the official flag of the Dominion of India on 15 August 1947. The flag was subsequently retained as that of the Republic of India. In India, the term "tricolour" (Hindi: तिरंगा, translit. Tiraṅgā) almost always refers to the Indian national flag. The flag is based on the Swaraj flag, a flag of the Indian National Congress designed by Pingali Venkayya.
[0.574, 0.233] Original true response: Independence and freedom, Gandhi and Nehru, developed democracy and democratic traditions. Adopted 24 August 1947 became the National flag of India, after a long period of evolution. The flag's tricolor design was adopted on 22 August 1947, when the Indian National Congress adopted it. The flag's design was first proposed by the Indian National Congress, and its first President, Pandit Jawaharlal Nehru, on 22 August 1889. The first National Flag was hoisted on 22 August 1308, when the Tata Steel and Tata Motors company's head office was shifted to Mumbai.
