[0.564, 0.135] Original true response: "Death penalty" was abolished in Oregon, by vote of the people in 1994 and has been enforced by law since 1995. Therefore, the death penalty was never reinstated, and the legislation was in effect until 2006. Voters in 2006 passed Ballot Measure 49, thereby prohibiting the death penalty. The Oregon capital punishment statutes were amended to remove the death penalty and allow the use of life imprisonment with no possibility of parole. The state has since, therefore, been using only life imprisonment. However, Oregon's death penalty was in place between 1994 and 2006. These were the only two years in which the death penalty was legal in Oregon.

Prompt: who is often associated with printing the first book using moveable type in germany
Ground truth: Johannes Gutenberg of Mainz, Germany is acknowledged as the first to invent a metal movable-type printing system in Europe, the printing press. Gutenberg was a goldsmith familiar with techniques of cutting punches for making coins from moulds. Between 1436 and 1450 he developed hardware and techniques for casting letters from matrices using a device called the hand mould. Gutenberg's key invention and contribution to movable-type printing in Europe, the hand mould, was the first practical means of making cheap copies of letterpunches in the vast quantities needed to print complete books, making the movable-type printing process a viable enterprise.
[0.590, 0.210] Original true response: To spread the spread of moveable-type printing, blocks of metal, wood or stone were made by printers. The technology of moveable-type printing was so valuable that a number of cities and countries, even China and the Islamic world, had developed it by the 1100s. The moveable-type printing press made it possible to mass-produce and distribute books on a scale never before imagined, and was a factor in the Reformation and the spread of literacy and education. The first book on moveable-type printing was created by Johannes Gutenberg, a German goldsmith and printer.

Prompt: what was the alliance between great britian russia and france known as
Ground truth: The Triple Entente (from French entente (ɑ̃tɑ̃t) "friendship, understanding, agreement") refers to the understanding linking the Russian Empire, the French Third Republic, and the United Kingdom of Great Britain and Ireland after the signing of the Anglo-Russian Entente on 31 August 1907. The understanding between the three powers, supplemented by agreements with Japan and Portugal, was a powerful counterweight to the Triple Alliance of Germany, Austria-Hungary, and Italy.
[0.500, 0.172] Original true response: During the war a treaty was signed between Britain and France. Although it was to be secret, and although it was leaked to the press, it was still widely reported. Britain's anti-French propaganda saw the alliance (often inaccurately) in terms of a Russian-French plot against Britain, and the French were resentful that Britain was not returning the property of French citizens that had been confiscated in 1793, 1803 and 1807. In the French view, Napoleon's pragmatic approach to the treaty was in contrast to that of the British, who, in an attempt to protect the wealthy Indian Ocean trade, and Britain's naval superiority, and the fear of Russian expansionism, were demanding restrictions that would severely limit Napoleon's ability to wage war.

Prompt: what do the 3 dots mean in math
Ground truth: In logical argument and mathematical proof, the therefore sign (∴) is generally used before a logical consequence, such as the conclusion of a syllogism. The symbol consists of three dots placed in an upright triangle and is read therefore. It is encoded at U + 2234 ∴ therefore (HTML & # 8756; &there4;). For common use in Microsoft Office hold the ALT key and type "8756". While it is not generally used in formal writing, it is used in mathematics and shorthand. It is complementary to U + 2235 ∵ because (HTML & # 8757;).
[0.487, 0.196] Original true response: In addition to also being the title of a book also covered in the  same chapter, the phrase appears in Euler's preface to his Analysis deskriptiven, but the main function of these equations is still not clear; in particular Euler's paper provides no proof for the value of the coefficients. This use of dots as an abbreviation of pi is said to be unique to the Analysis, but it is not clear how widely it was ever adopted. (In fact Euler did not use it; in his work the symbol π is used.) The importance of the third-dot notation is not emphasized in Euler's work.

Ground truth: Since 1947, there have been eight Chief Ministers of West Bengal. The first was Prafulla Chandra Ghosh of the Indian National Congress, who was succeeded by his party-mates Dr Bidhan Chandra Roy and Prafulla Chandra Sen. A decade of instability followed, marred by fractious coalition governments and frequent impositions of President's rule. The instability ended with 1977 election victory of the Communist Party of India (Marxist) (CPM). Headed by Jyoti Basu, the CPM-led Left Front government was in office for over 23 years (1977--2000). Left rule in West Bengal continued for another 10 years under Buddhadeb Bhattacharya, before its defeat in the 2011 election by the Trinamool Congress. Appointed on 20 May 2011, Trinamool leader Mamata Banerjee is the current incumbent, the state's first woman chief minister.
[0.555, 0.149] Original true response: 8 August 1947, West Bengal's first elected government, with Jawaharlal Nehru as Home Minister, created the province as an administrative unit of independent India, under the Bengal Reorganisation Act of 1947, splitting the Bengal Presidency into two states--West Bengal and East Bengal. On 21 August 1947, Dr. B.C. Roy became the first Chief Minister of West Bengal. In  1956, West Bengal became India's first state to adopt a new constitution, and Dr. B.C. Roy was re-elected as Chief Minister. Dr. B.C. Roy served as Chief Minister of West Bengal for a total of 10 years, from 21 August 1957 until 20 August 1962.

Prompt: what is the maximum data rate for the 802.11a standard select one
Ground truth: The 802.11 a standard uses the same data link layer protocol and frame format as the original standard, but an OFDM based air interface (physical layer). It operates in the 5 GHz band with a maximum net data rate of 54 Mbit/s, plus error correction code, which yields realistic net achievable throughput in the mid-20 Mbit/s.
[0.633, 0.224] Original true response: In 802.11b /g and n, the maximum data rate is 54 Mbps. At 5 GHz, 802.11n /ac /ad can reach 600 Mbps. While the 802.1 1a standard uses the 5 GHz frequency band, it does not support the maximum data rate of 600 Mbps as specified in the standard. Instead, the maximum data rate allowed is 26 Mbps. The 802.11 a standard also uses the 2.4 GHz frequency band, but it does not specify a maximum data rate.

Prompt: where does the term dog and pony show come from
Ground truth: The term was originally used in the United States in the late-19th and early-20th centuries to refer to small traveling circuses that toured through small towns and rural areas. The name derives from the common use of performing dogs and ponies as the main attractions of the events. Performances were generally held in open-air arenas, such as race tracks or public spaces in localities that were too small or remote to attract larger, more elaborate performers or performances. The most notorious was "Prof. Gentry's Famous Dog & Pony Show," started when teenager Henry Gentry and his brothers started touring in 1886 with their act, originally entitled "Gentry's Equine and Canine Paradox." It started small, but evolved into a full circus show. Other early dog and pony shows included Morris' Equine and Canine Paradoxes (1883) and Hurlburt's Dog and Pony Show (late 1880s).
[0.573, 0.202] Original true response: From the late 19th century--1800s--onwards. It is a slang term in the United States. It means a public relations stunt. This term originated from a vaudeville act: "dog and pony show" from the 1880s. This act was a comedy act featuring a dog and a pony. This act was performed on the stage. The dog and pony show was a stunt in which the dog and pony were shown to be untrainable and would not perform tricks. The dog and pony show ended with the dog and pony running off stage. The stunt was a huge success. The audience laughed at the antics of the dog and pony.

Prompt: who is recognized as the founder of islam
Ground truth: According to tradition, the Islamic prophet Muhammad was born in Mecca around the year 570. His family belonged to the Quraysh. When he was about forty years old, he began receiving what Muslims consider to be divine revelations delivered through the angel Gabriel, which would later form the Quran, enjoining him to proclaim a strict monotheistic faith, warn his compatriots of the impending Judgement Day, and castigate social injustices of his city. Muhammad's message won over a handful of followers and was met with increasing opposition from notables of Mecca. In 618, after he lost protection with the death of his influential uncle Abu Talib, Muhammad took flight to the city of Yathrib (subsequently called Medina) where he was joined by his followers. Later generations would count this event, known as the hijra, as the start of the Islamic era.

Prompt: who breaks a tie in the us senate
Ground truth: The Vice President of the United States is the ex officio President of the Senate, as provided in Article I, Section 3, Clause 4, of the United States Constitution, but may only vote in order to break a tie. According to the U.S. Senate, as of February 28, 2018, a tie-breaking vote had been cast 264 times by 36 vice presidents.
[0.578, 0.289] Original true response: By statute the Vice President of the United States is required to vote in the case of a tie in the Senate. When the Vice President is present in the Senate chamber (i.e. not present in the Vice Presidential office) he or she is required to vote in the same manner as any other Senator. This has been upheld in a number of Supreme Court decisions (e.g. United States v. Richardson (1823), 347 U.S. 48, 74 S.Ct. 389, 98 L.Ed. 430 (1954), although the Constitution does not explicitly state that the Vice President has a tie-breaking vote.

Prompt: where does us highway 1 start and end
Ground truth: U.S. Route 1 (US 1) is a major north--south U.S. Highway that serves the East Coast of the United States. It runs 2,369 miles (3,813 km), from Fort Kent, Maine, at the Canada--US border, south to Key West, Florida, making it the longest north--south road in the United States. US 1 is generally paralleled by Interstate 95 (I-95), though the former is significantly farther west (inland) between Jacksonville, Florida, and Petersburg, Virginia. The highway connects most of the major cities of the East Coast--including Miami, Jacksonville, Richmond, Washington, D.C., Baltimore, Philadelphia, New York City and Boston, passing from the Southeastern United States to New England.
[0.656, 0.306] Original true response: US Route 1, the Atlantic Coast Road, is the easternmost north-south U.S. Highway. The northernmost point of US Route 1 is located in Maine; the southernmost point of US Route 1, the easternmost U.S. highway, is located in Key West, Florida, while it is the westernmost U.S. highway to cross the Atlantic Ocean. It is the only major east-west road connecting the northeastern United States with the Florida peninsula, with a total length of 3,073 miles. It is a major artery of the East Coast of the United States, with 14 states it passes through.

Prompt: first jnanpith award was an autor of which language
Ground truth: G. Sankara Kurup, (3 June 1901, Nayathode, Kingdom of Cochin (now in Ernakulam district, Kerala, India)--2 February 1978, Vappalassery, Angamaly, Ernakulam district, Kerala), better known as Mahakavi G (The Great Poet G), was the first winner of the Jnanpith Award, India's highest literary award. He won the prize in 1965 for his collection of poems in Malayalam Odakkuzhal (The Bamboo Flute, 1950). With part of the prize money he established the literary award Odakkuzhal in 1968. He was also the recipient of the Soviet Land Nehru Award, in 1967, and the Padma Bhushan in 1968. His poetry collection Viswadarshanam won the Kerala Sahitya Akademi Award in 1961 and Kendra Sahitya Akademi Award in 1963.
[0.632, 0.310] Original true response: The Jnanpith Award, India's highest literary award, recognizes literary works in 24 Indian languages. Its recipients include Kannada poet and novelist U.R. Ananthamurthy (2002). He was awarded the 28th Jnanpith Award in 2002 for his novel Bharatiya Vidya Bhavana, Kannada Sahitya Parishat published in 1993. He was also awarded the Padma Bhushan in 1981 and the Padma Vibhushan in 2014. He was awarded the Sahitya Akademi Award in 1966,  1972 and 1978. He was awarded the M.M. Chaturvedi Award in 1989.

Prompt: what does it mean to be on parole
Ground truth: Parole is a temporary release of a prisoner who agrees to certain conditions before the completion of the maximum sentence period, originating from the French parole ("voice, spoken words"). The term became associated during the Middle Ages with the release of prisoners who gave their word.
[0.547, 0.127] Original true response: That he or she must abide by specific rules or restrictions for specified period of time, or complete certain tasks as mandated by a parole board (in some jurisdictions known as a "probation board") after being released from a prison. In the United States it is usually used for release of inmates after serving part of their sentence. Most parolees are released to supervision by the state, and in that sense "under parole supervision". It is not as restrictive as probation, in that parolees can leave the state for limited periods of time, and in most cases it is not known by the parolee that he or she is under parole.

Prompt: the origins of the stations of the cross
Ground truth: The Stations of the Cross or the Way of the Cross, also known as the Way of Sorrows or the Via Crucis, refers to a series of images depicting Jesus Christ on the day of his crucifixion and accompanying prayers. The stations grew out of imitations of Via Dolorosa in Jerusalem which is believed to be the actual path Jesus walked to Mount Calvary. The object of the stations is to help the Christians faithful to make a spiritual pilgrimage through contemplation of the Passion of Christ. It has become one of the most popular devotions and the stations can be found in many Western Christian churches, including Anglican, Lutheran, Methodist, and Roman Catholic ones.
[0.639, 0.303] Original true response: Of the more than 140 stations of the cross to be found in the Christian tradition of the Via Dolorosa in Jerusalem the current stations are the result of the 1969 revision of the stations of the cross as set forth by the Catholic Church, although the original 14 stations are still used in some traditions. The Via Dolorosa (or Way of Sorrows) is a route in Jerusalem, considered to be the path followed by Jesus Christ (and the Virgin Mary) on the day of the crucifixion, and the stations of the cross are a commemoration of the various points along the way where the Gospel accounts of the Passion of Jesus describe a specific event.

[0.409, 0.079] Original true response: In wet years-after heavy rainfalls-water flowed through rivers, eventually filling Lake Eyre. During its flow through the desert regions it dissolved salts to the extent of about 25 parts per thousand. The high concentration of salt in the water entering the lake would result in the formation of evaporites (precipitates of salt) in the bottom of the lake. After the water evaporated from the lake (during the dry seasons), the salt would remain in the bottom of the lake. The lake would be refilled by the inflow of the water with a high concentration of salt, and the process would repeat.

Prompt: when does a limit of a function not exist
Ground truth: Formal definitions, first devised in the early 19th century, are given below. Informally, a function f assigns an output f (x) to every input x. We say the function has a limit L at an input p: this means f (x) gets closer and closer to L as x moves closer and closer to p. More specifically, when f is applied to any input sufficiently close to p, the output value is forced arbitrarily close to L. On the other hand, if some inputs very close to p are taken to outputs that stay a fixed distance apart, we say the limit does not exist.
[0.503, 0.100] Original true response: As an infinite sequence increases, as an infinite series diverges, as a function grows by taking on larger values (infinite), as a limit of a function exists, the function can take on an infinite number of values, the limit of a function exists. The limit of a function can be found by using the definition of the derivative (in calculus), by using the definition of the integral (in calculus), by using the method of infinite series (in calculus), or by using the method of Taylor series (in calculus). No mathematical or logical inconsistencies are implied in the above definition, but a finite number of values (i.e., the number of values of the function).

It also appears in the Flag of Argentina and the Coat of Arms of Bolivia.

Prompt: when did beryl markham fly across the atlantic
Ground truth: When Markham decided to take on the Atlantic crossing, no female pilot had yet flown non-stop from Europe to New York, and no woman had made the westward flight solo, though several had died trying. Markham hoped to claim both records. On 4 September 1936, she took off from Abingdon, England. After a 20-hour flight, her Vega Gull, The Messenger, suffered fuel starvation due to icing of the fuel tank vents, and she crash-landed at Baleine Cove on Cape Breton Island, Nova Scotia, Canada. She became the first person to make it from England to North America non-stop from east to west. She was celebrated as an aviation pioneer.
[0.597, 0.199] Original true response: Because of her childhood experiences with horses, Markham developed a passion for flying. She had a custom-built plane, the Westland, and on 20 July 1932 became the first woman to fly solo across the Atlantic, from east to west. She also set the first woman transatlantic flight record. Markham made a second Atlantic crossing (from east to west) on 1933. The first female pilot to cross the Atlantic solo was Amy Johnson. She completed the journey on 9 May 1930. Markham also became the first woman to fly solo from Cape Town to London, a distance of 1,600 miles (2,600 km), on 17 August 1933.

Prompt: where does eye of the round roast come from
Ground truth: A round steak is a beef steak from the "round", the rear leg of the cow. The round is divided into cuts including the eye (of) round, bottom round, and top round, with or without the "round" bone (femur), and may include the knuckle (sirloin tip), depending on how the round is separated from the loin. This is a lean cut and it is moderately tough. Lack of fat and marbling makes round dry out when cooked with dry-heat cooking methods like roasting or grilling. Round steak is commonly prepared with slow moist-heat methods including braising, to tenderize the meat and maintain moisture. The cut is often sliced thin, then dried or smoked at low temperature to make jerky.
[0.643, 0.277] Original true response: A round roast is a cut of beef from the short loin, consisting of the eye and the tenderloin. The "eye" of the roast (sometimes spelled "eye roast" or "eye steak") is the cut of meat from the eye of the animal (or the rear of the animal), including the eyeball and the surrounding tissue. This is considered a premium cut of meat and is usually lean and tender, with a distinctive flavor from the short loin and the marbling of fat in the tissue. A well-cooked eye roast can be a juicy and flavorful centerpiece for a dinner party, and is often served with vegetables and a gravy.

Prompt: where do bullets go when fired in the air
Ground truth: Bullets fired into the air usually fall back with terminal velocities much lower than their muzzle velocity when they leave the barrel of a firearm. Nevertheless, people can be injured, sometimes fatally, when bullets discharged into the air fall back down to the ground. Bullets fired at angles less than vertical are more dangerous as the bullet maintains its angular ballistic trajectory and is far less likely to engage in tumbling motion; it therefore travels at speeds much higher than a bullet in free fall.
[0.585, 0.185] Original true response: Bullets usually lose enough velocity when they penetrate the atmosphere that they can not be seen with the naked eye even if they are still airborne. When a bullet is fired at a velocity of about 1,200 mph, its air resistance is so great that it is almost impossible to maintain a stable trajectory; therefore, it is often necessary to use a trajectory chart to predict the trajectory of a bullet. The charts are prepared for a particular caliber of firearm and are the only means by which a shooter can accurately predict the trajectory of his bullets.

Prompt: when does canada switch to daylight savings time
Ground truth: In regions where daylight saving time is used, it commences on the second Sunday of March, and standard time restarts on the first Sunday in November.
[0.480, 0.134] Original true response: Daylight saving time in Canada takes place in accordance with Canada Time Act (Statutes of Canada, 1970, ch.  14 (2nd Supplement)), which allows most provinces and territories to switch between standard and daylight time. The Act specifies the dates (dates) when most provinces and territories (with the exception of Saskatchewan and parts of Quebec) must switch between standard and daylight time (although the dates may vary slightly, due to the different time zones). However, there are two exceptions: Saskatchewan and parts thereof are exempt from observing daylight saving time, and the Yukon and Northwest Territories are also exempt, though the latter two do observe most other federal regulations.

Ground truth: ICQ is an instant messaging client that was first developed and popularized by the Israeli company Mirabilis in 1996. The name ICQ derives from the English phrase "I Seek You". Ownership of ICQ passed from Mirabilis to AOL in 1998, and from AOL to Mail.Ru Group in 2010.
[0.475, 0.160] Original true response: ICQ enabled its users to send instant messages to others. Whenever a message was received (either from another ICQ user, or from a system message), ICQ would display a small icon (indicating the type of message, such as "New Instant Message", "Delivery Failure", etc.) in the bottom-left corner of the screen. Users could respond to the messages by clicking on the icon, which opened the ICQ message window. The messaging system supported a number of features, such as the ability to send files (through the use of attachments), and the ability to specify a maximum size limit to the message.

Prompt: what was the alliance between great britian russia and france known as
Ground truth: The Triple Entente (from French entente (ɑ̃tɑ̃t) "friendship, understanding, agreement") refers to the understanding linking the Russian Empire, the French Third Republic, and the United Kingdom of Great Britain and Ireland after the signing of the Anglo-Russian Entente on 31 August 1907. The understanding between the three powers, supplemented by agreements with Japan and Portugal, was a powerful counterweight to the Triple Alliance of Germany, Austria-Hungary, and Italy.
[0.535, 0.172] Original true response: The Treaty of Alliance and the Anglo-French Alliance were mutual defense treaties between Britain and France. Although the two were not formally allied until 1904, the two countries had cooperated on many military operations during the Crimean War. The treaty was also violated with both nations having secretly armed the Serbs against the Ottoman Empire in the Balkan Wars. It was also violated in World War I, as the French failed to fulfill their pledges to the British in 1915. The failure of the treaty was caused by the mutual distrust of the two nations. The two nations did not have a long-term alliance, and the alliance was not renewed after the war.

Prompt: chandra and bhaga river meets at the place
Ground truth: The Chandrabhaga or Chenab (Vedic name Askni), the largest river (in terms of volume of water) is formed after the meeting of two streams namely, Chandra and Bhaga at Tandi, in Lahaul. It flows 122 kilometres (76 mi) and covers an area of 7,500 square kilometres (2,900 sq mi). in Himachal, before entering Kashmir. The Chandra passes through the barren tribal land.
[0.546, 0.158] Original true response: The Ganges and the Yamuna rivers. The Yamuna is sometimes referred to as the Yamu or Jamuna, while the Ganges is sometimes referred to as the Ganga. The Ganges is the longer of the two rivers, at 2,525 km (1,570 mi), while the Yamuna is 1,376 km (855 mi). The Ganges is wider, at  200 m (660 ft), while the Yamuna is 80 m (262 ft). The Ganges is considered holy in Hinduism, and is often used for bathing, washing, and burial in Hindu culture.

Prompt: when was the death penalty reinstated in oregon
Ground truth: In 1984, Measure 6 amended the state constitution to once more make the death penalty legal. Measure 7, a statutory measure passed in the same year, required a separate sentencing hearing before a jury in cases of aggravated murder.
[0.548, 0.133] Original true response: "Death penalty" was never abolished in Oregon, and therefore has never been reinstated. The death penalty was first introduced in 1855, and was upheld by Oregon voters in  1994. Since then, the Oregon Supreme Court has struck down the death penalty three times, in 1997,  2001 and 2006. However, in 2007, the Oregon Legislature reinstated the death penalty. Since then, the state has executed one person, Gary Haugen, in 1997. However, in 2011, the state legislature passed a bill, SB 494, which was signed into law by Governor John Kitzhaber, abolishing the death penalty in Oregon.

Prompt: when was the national flag of india adopted
Ground truth: The National Flag of India is a horizontal rectangular tricolour of India saffron, white and India green; with the Ashoka Chakra, a 24-spoke wheel, in navy blue at its centre. It was adopted in its present form during a meeting of the Constituent Assembly held on 22 July 1947, and it became the official flag of the Dominion of India on 15 August 1947. The flag was subsequently retained as that of the Republic of India. In India, the term "tricolour" (Hindi: तिरंगा, translit. Tiraṅgā) almost always refers to the Indian national flag. The flag is based on the Swaraj flag, a flag of the Indian National Congress designed by Pingali Venkayya.
[0.569, 0.301] Original true response: Adopted, official and civil ensigns, and national flag were approved and adopted by the Constituent Assembly on 22 July 1947. The flag was adopted on 15 August 1947, the day that India became independent. The flag's red, yellow, and green colors were chosen because they were the colors of the Indian National Congress; the white stripe, on the other hand, was a compromise between the Congress and the Muslim League. The Indian National Congress and the Muslim League were the two major political parties that fought the Indian independence movement; the Muslim League was led by Jinnah and the Congress Party was led by Gandhi.

Prompt: who is often associated with printing the first book using moveable type in germany
Ground truth: Johannes Gutenberg of Mainz, Germany is acknowledged as the first to invent a metal movable-type printing system in Europe, the printing press. Gutenberg was a goldsmith familiar with techniques of cutting punches for making coins from moulds. Between 1436 and 1450 he developed hardware and techniques for casting letters from matrices using a device called the hand mould. Gutenberg's key invention and contribution to movable-type printing in Europe, the hand mould, was the first practical means of making cheap copies of letterpunches in the vast quantities needed to print complete books, making the movable-type printing process a viable enterprise.
[0.570, 0.206] Original true response: To illustrate the new invention, Gutenberg created a series of small, movable metal pieces--letters, spaces, and punctuation marks. Because these pieces were arranged and attached to a metal spine, they could be rearranged and reused; and because they were made of metal, they could be moved easily and quickly. The result was a printing press with a high level of quality and speed; and the movable-type printing press was the key to Gutenbergs invention. The metal pieces were made from a soft metal, and Gutenbergs printers were able to use them with a specially-designed ink and a press.

Prompt: who won the battle of stirling bridge 1297
Ground truth: The Battle of Stirling Bridge was a battle of the First War of Scottish Independence. On 11 September 1297, the forces of Andrew Moray and William Wallace defeated the combined English forces of John de Warenne, 6th Earl of Surrey, and Hugh de Cressingham near Stirling, on the River Forth.
[0.515, 0.204] Original true response: The English army under the command of Robert Bruce was victorious in the Battle of Stirling Bridge with the loss of some 500 men: it was a heavy defeat for the English with some 1,500 men killed. The Scots were said to have numbered some 10,000. The English army was almost wiped out, and the Scots were said to have suffered only 500 casualties. The English army was largely wiped out, and the English were defeated. It was the only major victory of the war.

Ground truth: The Traveling Wilburys (sometimes shortened to the Wilburys) were a British-American supergroup consisting of Bob Dylan, George Harrison, Jeff Lynne, Roy Orbison, and Tom Petty. The band recorded two albums, the first in 1988 and the second in 1990, though Orbison died before the second was recorded.
[0.627, 0.274] Original true response: Traveling Wilburys Trademark The Traveling Wilburys logo, a cartoon of the five members (clockwise from top-left): Bob Dylan, George Harrison, Jeff Lynne, Roy Orbison, Tom Petty, in the mid-1970s The Traveling Wilburys were a supergroup of musicians who came together to record a single album, Traveling Wilburys Vol. 1 (1976), a studio album consisting of five songs. Though an unintended success, the album was a chart-topper in the United States and the United Kingdom. Despite the success, the group disbanded after the death of Roy Orbison in 1980. Prompt: what do the 3 dots mean in math
Ground truth: In logical argument and mathematical proof, the therefore sign (∴) is generally used before a logical consequence, such as the conclusion of a syllogism. The symbol consists of three dots placed in an upright triangle and is read therefore. It is encoded at U + 2234 ∴ therefore (HTML & # 8756; &there4;). For common use in Microsoft Office hold the ALT key and type "8756". While it is not generally used in formal writing, it is used in mathematics and shorthand. It is complementary to U + 2235 ∵ because (HTML & # 8757;).
[0.502, 0.188] Original true response: In addition to the also common usage as a "shortcut" in computer applications, the use of three dots in mathematics has been explained as follows: In the middle of the 19th century, John Tate introduced the notation of three dots to represent infinite groups; he used this notation in his 1859 publication A Treatise on the Theory of Algebraic Equations. This is the earliest known usage of the three dots (.:. :.) in mathematics. Tate used the notation to represent the fact that a particular element in the group is not specified.
